Kafa'a for Non-Arabs: The Three Bases and the Piety Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether piety is a kafa'a factor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three bases of kafa'a among non-Arabs and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over piety, identifying the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
For non-Arabs, kafa'a hinges on Islam, freedom, and piety; piety is the contested element.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah, we explore how the Hanafi school defines marital compatibility (kafa'a) for non-Arabs ('ajam). Unlike Arabs, lineage is replaced by three specific criteria: Islam, freedom, and piety. The most nuanced criterion is piety, which sparked an internal debate between Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani? Actually 'al-sahiban' refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but here the text says 'lahuma' which likely refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? However, the commentary clarifies: 'lahuma' means the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Understanding this dispute helps us see how Hanafi jurists balance textual evidence (Quran 49:13) with practical reasoning (social shame). The relied-upon view considers piety, making it a valid basis for a woman to refuse an impious husband.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
What is the passage addressing? It explains the criteria for marriage compatibility specifically for non-Arabs, where lineage is not considered.
2
Extract the Three Bases
List the three bases: Islam (with gradations based on number of Muslim parents/ancestors), freedom (slave or freedman is not equal to free-born), and piety (diyana).
3
Locate the Dispute
The dispute is solely about piety: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani argues it should not be considered except in extreme cases (public drunkenness, mockery). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue it should be a general consideration.
Intra-Hanafi: both sides are from the Hanafi school.
4
Examine the Proofs
Imam Muhammad's proof: Piety is a matter of the Hereafter, so worldly rulings (like marriage) should not be based on it unless the husband's immorality is publicly disgraceful. The two's proof: Quran 49:13 (nobility by piety) and that a woman is more shamed by a husband's immorality than by his lineage.
The verse is the primary textual proof.
5
Determine the Tarjih
The passage states 'this is the correct view' referring to the view of the two. The commentary confirms that the relied-upon position is that piety is a valid basis for kafa'a among non-Arabs.
Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah declares the view of the two as sahih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ
وفي العجم إسلاماً، فذو أبوين في الإسلام كفو لذي آباء فيه، ومسلم بنفسه غيرُ كفؤ لذي أب فيه، ولا ذو أب فيه لذي أبوين فيه، وحرية فليس عبد، أو معتق كفؤاً لحرّة أصلية، ولا معتق أبوه كفؤاً لذات أبوين حرين. وديانة وفيه خلاف محمد الله هو يقول : التدين من الأمور التي تظهر ثمرتُها في الآخرة، فلا تبتني أحكام الدنيا عليه إلا أن يكون الزوج بفسقه بحيث يسخر منه أو يخرج إلى الأسواق وهو سكران. ولهما : إن التقوى من أعلى المفاخر يدل عليه قوله تعالى {إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ اللَّهِ أَنْقَكُمْ} الآية، والمرأة تُعير بفسق الزوج فوق التعير بالنسب، فوجب اعتبارها، وهذا هو الصحيح. كذا في «الهداية» وهذا كله في العجم، وأما العرب فقيل : لا تعتبر فيهم هذه الكفاءة، كما مرَّ نقله، والصحيح من المذهب اعتبارها فيهما كذا في إيضاح الاصلاح» و«النهر» و«البحر».
The Book of Marriage, Chapter of the Guardian and Compatibility. And among non-Arabs, in terms of Islam: one with both parents in Islam is compatible for one with grandfathers in Islam; one who embraced Islam himself but whose parents are not Muslims is not compatible for one with one parent in Islam; nor is one with one parent in Islam compatible for one with both parents in Islam. And in terms of freedom: a slave or an emancipated slave is not compatible for a free-born woman; nor is one whose father was emancipated compatible for a woman whose both parents are free. And in terms of piety. There is a disagreement: Muhammad (al-Shaybani) says that religiousness is among those matters whose fruit appears in the Hereafter, so worldly rulings are not built upon it unless the husband's immorality is such that he is mocked or goes out to markets while drunk. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: piety is among the highest honors; evidence for this is His statement {Indeed, the noblest of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you} the verse. A woman is shamed by the husband's immorality more than by lineage, so it must be considered. This is the correct view. This is in al-Hidayah. All this is regarding non-Arabs. As for Arabs, it is said that these considerations are not observed among them, as previously quoted; but the sound position in the madhhab is that they are observed in both (Arabs and non-Arabs). This is in Idah al-Islah, al-Nahr, and al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah deals with compatibility (kafa'a) in marriage specifically for non-Arabs ('ajam). The author explains that among non-Arabs, lineage (nasab) is not considered because they have neglected their genealogies. Instead, compatibility is assessed on three bases: Islam, freedom, and piety.

First, regarding Islam: A man whose father and grandfather are both Muslim is compatible for a woman whose father and grandfather are Muslim. But a man who embraced Islam himself while his parents were non-Muslim is not compatible for a woman with even one Muslim parent. Similarly, a man with only one Muslim parent is not compatible for a woman with two Muslim parents.

Second, regarding freedom: A slave or an emancipated slave is not compatible for a free-born woman whose lineage is free. Likewise, a man whose father was emancipated is not compatible for a woman whose both parents are free-born. This is because slavery is a mark of humiliation.

Third, regarding piety (diyana): Here there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that piety is not a basis for worldly rulings like marriage compatibility, because its full reward is in the Hereafter. The only exception is if the husband's immorality is so extreme that he becomes a public mockery or is seen drunk in the markets. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani? Actually 'lahuma' here refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, but Muhammad is part of 'lahuma'? In this context, 'lahuma' refers to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? Wait: In the Hidayah, 'lahuma' often refers to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). But here the text says 'lahuma' and then cites the verse, and concludes 'this is the correct view'. The commentary states that the two argue that piety is the highest honor, supported by Quran 49:13, and that a woman is more shamed by her husband's immorality than by his low lineage, so piety must be considered. The author then declares this view as sound (sahih).

Finally, the passage notes that all these considerations are for non-Arabs. For Arabs, some scholars say these do not apply because Arab lineage is preserved, but the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that these criteria are also considered among Arabs.

Key Terms
الكفاءة (al-kafā’a): Equality or compatibility in marriage between spouses in certain attributes such that the marriage is considered suitable.
العجم (al-‘ajam): Non-Arabs, i.e., those who are not of Arab lineage, regardless of language.
الديانة (al-diyāna): Religiousness, piety, and moral uprightness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view in this passage, is piety (diyana) considered as a factor for marriage compatibility among non-Arabs?
A) No, because piety is a matter of the Hereafter.
B) Yes, because a woman is shamed by her husband's immorality more than by his lineage.
C) Only if the husband is a public drunk or mocked.
D) No, only freedom and Islam are considered.

Key Takeaway
For non-Arabs, kafa'a is based on Islam (graded), freedom, and piety; the latter is confirmed by the Sahibayn as a valid consideration.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
12 min
Kafa'ah in Marriage: Freedom, Piety, and Wealth
Rulings on compatibility factors in Hanafi marriage law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criteria for kafa'ah (freedom, piety, wealth) and apply them to determine whether a marriage is compatible, including identifying the relied-upon position on diyanah.

Core Idea
A slave is not compatible with a free-born woman; a sinful man not with a pious woman; a pauper not with a poor woman.

Marriage compatibility (kafa'ah) in Hanafi law protects the woman and her family from social disgrace. This lesson examines three key factors: freedom, religious piety, and financial ability. You will learn the rules, the proof, and the relied-upon position, including an important intra-Hanafi dispute on whether piety should be considered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three factors
The passage discusses kafa'ah in terms of freedom (hurriyyah), religion/piety (diyanah), and wealth (mal). List them and note their sequence in the text.
Freedom is presented first, then piety, then wealth.
2
Analyze freedom rulings
Determine who is compatible with whom regarding freedom: a slave or freedman is not compatible with a free-born woman (hurrah asliyyah); a man whose father was freed is not compatible with a woman whose both parents are free.
Freedman is compatible with freedwoman, but low-born freedman not with noble freedwoman.
3
Examine the diyanah dispute
Note the disagreement: Abu Hanifa did not consider piety as a factor, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argued it is based on Quran 49:13. Identify which position is mufta bihi.
The relied-upon position is to consider diyanah, as stated in al-Hidayah.
4
Understand the wealth factor
A man unable to pay the prompt dowry (mahr mu'ajjal) and maintenance (nafaqah) is not compatible with a poor woman. This ties financial capability to compatibility.
Financial inability breaks compatibility only if the woman is poor (faqirah); if she is wealthy, different ruling may apply.
5
Apply the rules to a scenario
Given a marriage proposal between a pious free-born woman and a freedman who is openly fasiq and poor, determine if the guardian can object. Apply all three factors.
The marriage is valid but guardian can object due to lack of compatibility on all counts.

(ومالا (٤) [١] قوله : لبنت الصالح ; ظاهره أن العبرة لصلاح الأب والجد وإن كانت هي فاسقة، واعتبر في «المجمع صلاحها حيث قال : لا يكون الفاسق كفؤاً للصالحة. واعتبر في «الخانية» صلاح الكلِّ حيث قال : لا يكون الفاسقُ كفؤاً للصالحة بنت الصالحين. والحق أن المعتبر صلاح الكلِّ ومَن اقتصر على صلاحها، أو صلاح أبيها نظر إلى الغالب من أن صلاح الوالد والولد متلازمان فالفاسق ليس كفؤا للصالحة بنت الصالح، بل لفاسقة بنت فاسق ، وكذا الفاسقة بنت صالح فليس لأبيها حق الاعتراض ; لأن ما يلحقه من العار ببنته فوق ما يلحقه بصهره ، وأما إذا كانت صالحة بنت فاسق فزوجت نفسها من فاسق فليس له حق الاعتراض ; لأنه مثله ، وهي قد رضيت به. كما حققه في رد المحتار» [٢] قوله : وإن لم; الواو :وصليه : أي وإن لم يكن الفاسق معلناً : أي جاهراً به ومظهراً، وهذا هو الظاهر ; لأنّ العار بالفسق غير مقتصر على الإعلان. كذا في النهر وغيره. [٣] قوله : وعند بعض المشايخ ; المقصود منه ذكر إفادة تقييد المصنف له حكم التعميم بقوله : «في اختيار الفضلي». [٤] قوله : ومالاً; عطف على قوله : «إسلاماً» كقوله الماضي: «ديانة»، وقوله الآتي: «حرفة»، وقوله الماضي : «حرية) ; وظاهر كلام المصنف له أن هذه الكفاءات يختص اعتبارها في العجم، وهو قول لبعضهم، كما مرَّ نقله عن «جامع الرموز» ومر ذكر ما فيه، وسيأتي بعضه. فالعاجر عن المهر المعجل والنفقة ليس كفؤاً للفقيرة
Thus a slave or a freedman is not a compatible match for a free-born woman, nor is a person whose father is freed a compatible match for a woman with both free parents. And [compatibility in] religion: [the text:] Islam and according to freedom, and that is not considered among the Arabs, because freedom is necessary for them since the Arabs cannot be enslaved, and likewise there is no boasting among them in Islam, so an Arab whose father is a disbeliever is a compatible match for an Arab woman whose ancestors are in Islam. [1] His saying: 'compatible' because slavery is an effect of disbelief and has a meaning of lowliness. The free-born woman (hurrah asliyyah) is one whom slavery has not reached herself nor her ancestors; she refuses to be a bedfellow to one whom slavery has reached himself or his origins. Thus a freedman himself or a slave is not compatible for her, and likewise one whose father is freed is not compatible for one whose both parents are free. And a freedman is compatible for a freedwoman; however, a low-born freedman is not compatible for a noble freedwoman. As in al-Dhakhirah. [2] His saying: 'and religion' meaning compatibility is considered according to religion, which (with kasra) is expression of piety, righteousness, and noble character, and regarding this there is a disagreement with Muhammad al-Shaybani who said: religiousness is among matters whose fruit appears in the hereafter, so worldly rulings are not based on it unless the husband is by his sin such that he is mocked or goes out to the markets drunk. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue: piety is the highest of distinctions, evidenced by His statement: 'O mankind, indeed We have created you from male and female and made you peoples and tribes that you may know one another. Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you' (49:13) and a woman is more disgraced by the sin of her husband than by lineage, so it must be considered. And this is the correct view. As in al-Hidayah. And all this applies to non-Arabs; as for Arabs, it was said that this compatibility is not considered among them, as previously transmitted, and the correct position in the school is to consider it in both, as in Ihya' al-Islah, al-Nahr, and al-Bahr. The Book of Nikah, Section on the Guardian and Compatibility: So a sinful man (fasiq) is not a compatible match for the daughter of a righteous man, even if he does not announce [his sin] in the matter of the woman's choice of the best in religion and wealth. And according to some scholars: if the sinful man does not announce [his sin], he is a compatible match for the daughter of a righteous man. (4) [1] His saying: 'for the daughter of a righteous man' – its apparent meaning is that consideration is given to the righteousness of the father and grandfather even if she is sinful. And in al-Majma' it is considered based on her righteousness where it says: the sinful man is not compatible for the righteous woman. And in al-Khaniyyah it considers the righteousness of all where it says: the sinful man is not compatible for the righteous woman who is the daughter of righteous parents. The truth is that the considered factor is the righteousness of all. And whoever restricts it to her righteousness or her father's righteousness looks to the general rule that the righteousness of parent and child are interconnected. So a sinful man is not compatible for a righteous woman who is the daughter of a righteous man, but rather for a sinful woman who is the daughter of a sinful man. Similarly, if a sinful woman is the daughter of a righteous man, her father has no right to object because the disgrace that befalls him through his daughter is greater than that through his son-in-law. And if she is a righteous woman daughter of a sinful man and she marries herself to a sinful man, he has no right to object because [the man] is like him, and she is pleased with him. As verified in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His saying: 'even if he does not announce' – the waw is for conciliation, meaning even if the sinful man is not open, i.e., not publicizing and manifesting [his sin]. And this is apparent because the disgrace from sin is not limited to openness. As in al-Nahr and others. [3] His saying: 'and according to some scholars' – the purpose is to mention the benefit of the author's restriction of the ruling by his saying 'in the matter of the woman's choice of the best'. [4] His saying: 'and wealth' – it is coordinated with his saying 'Islam', like his previous 'religion' and his forthcoming 'profession', and his previous 'freedom'. And the apparent meaning of the author's statement is that these compatibilities are considered only among non-Arabs, and that is the saying of some of them, as previously transmitted from Jami' al-Rumuz, and mention of what is in it has passed, and some of it will come. So the one who is unable to pay the prompt dowry and maintenance is not a compatible match for a poor woman.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi rulings on kafa'ah (marital compatibility) from the commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. It covers three main factors: freedom (hurriyyah), religious piety (diyanah), and wealth (mal).

The first part deals with freedom: a slave or a freedman is not compatible with a free-born woman whose ancestors have never been enslaved. The commentary explains that slavery has an element of lowliness derived from disbelief, and a free-born woman would be disgraced by such a match. However, a freedman is compatible with a freedwoman, but a low-born freedman is not compatible with a noble freedwoman.

Regarding religiosity (diyanah), the text mentions a significant intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa reportedly did not consider piety as a factor in compatibility, because its effects are in the afterlife. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) argue that piety is the highest form of nobility, citing the Quranic verse (49:13) 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous of you.' They note that a woman's disgrace from her husband's sin is greater than from his lineage. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that piety is considered, as stated in al-Hidayah. The text also debates whether the righteousness of the woman herself, her father, or both is considered; the correct view is that it is the righteousness of all.

All these rulings generally apply to non-Arabs, but for Arabs there is a difference of opinion; the correct position is that they are also considered among Arabs.

The final section addresses wealth: a man unable to pay the prompt (mu'ajjal) dowry and provide maintenance is not compatible with a poor woman. This ties compatibility to financial capability.

Throughout, the emphasis is on the practical effect of compatibility: it gives the woman's guardian (wali) the right to object to a marriage that is not compatible according to these criteria.

Key Terms
كفؤ (kufu'): Compatible match in marriage; a man who is equal to the woman in certain criteria such as lineage, freedom, religion, wealth, and profession.
حرّة أصلية (hurrah asliyyah): A free-born woman who has never been enslaved herself and whose ancestors have never been enslaved.
ديانة (diyanah): Religious piety; the quality of being devout and righteous, considered as a factor in marital compatibility.
فاسق (fasiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; one who is openly sinful.
صالحة (salihah): A righteous woman who is pious and avoids sins.
عاجر عن المهر المعجل والنفقة ('ajiz 'an al-mahr al-mu'ajjal wa al-nafaqah): One who is unable to pay the prompt dowry (mahr mu'ajjal) and provide maintenance (nafaqah); such a person is not compatible with a poor woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) regarding diyanah (religious piety) in kafa'ah according to this passage?
A) Diyanah is not considered at all because it is purely a matter of the afterlife.
B) Diyanah is considered only if the husband's sinfulness is open and notorious.
C) Diyanah is considered, based on the Quranic verse emphasizing taqwa, and this is the correct view.
D) Diyanah is considered only among non-Arabs, not among Arabs.

Key Takeaway
Kafa'ah in Hanafi law protects the woman and her family from social disgrace through considerations of freedom, piety, and wealth, with the right of objection vesting in the guardian; the marriage itself remains valid even if incompatible.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Fasiq as a Suitable Match in Marriage
Ruling and commentary on fasiq being non-kuf' for pious woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the kafa'ah of a fasiq for a pious woman and identify the conditions and disagreements that affect suitability.

Core Idea
A fasiq is never kuf' for a pious woman, even if he hides his sin; hidden sin still disgraces.

Marriage suitability (kafa'ah) is a key consideration in Hanafi marriage law. One of its criteria is religiosity (din). This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses a specific question: is a sinful man (fasiq) an equal match for a righteous woman? The answer has practical consequences for a guardian's right to object. We will unpack the ruling, its condition regarding open sinning, and an internal Hanafi disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Base Ruling
Identify the central ruling: a fasiq is not a suitable match (kuf') for the daughter of a righteous man. Note that this applies even if the fasiq does not openly sin.
The matn of al-Wiqaya is clear on this.
2
Clarify 'Daughter of the Righteous'
Analyze whose righteousness matters. The text discusses whether it is the father's, the woman's, or both. The correct view (al-haqq) is that all are considered, due to presumed interdependence of righteousness between parent and child.
This affects cases where the woman herself is sinful or her father is sinful.
3
Evaluate the Condition of Open Sinning
The phrase 'even if he does not openly sin' shows that hidden sin is also disqualifying. The disgrace of sin is inherent, not dependent on publicity. This is the relied-upon position.
Some scholars disagree, but the majority view is as stated.
4
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Some Hanafi scholars hold that a fasiq who does not openly sin is kuf' for the daughter of a righteous man. Their reasoning is that hidden sin causes less shame. The text rebuts this, affirming the general ruling.
The dispute is legitimate; the mufta bihi is the non-kuf' position.
5
Apply the Ruling to Concrete Scenarios
Work through different combinations: pious woman/pious father, sinful woman/righteous father, pious woman/sinful father, etc. Determine when the guardian has a right to object.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is crucial for fatwa.

[٣] قوله : وعند بعض المشايخ ؛ المقصود منه ذكر إفادة تقييد المصنف له حكم التعميم بقوله : «في اختيار الفضلي».
A sinful man is not a suitable match for the daughter of a righteous man, even if he does not openly sin in choosing the most excellent in religion and wealth. And according to some scholars: 'The sinful man, if he does not openly sin, is a suitable match for the daughter of a righteous man.' [1] His statement: 'for the daughter of the righteous' – its apparent meaning is that the consideration is for the righteousness of the father and grandfather, even if she herself is sinful. And in al-Majma', it is considered her righteousness, where it says: 'A sinful man is not a suitable match for a righteous woman.' And in al-Khaniyya, it is considered the righteousness of all, where it says: 'A sinful man is not a suitable match for a righteous woman who is the daughter of righteous men.' The truth is that what is considered is the righteousness of all. Whoever restricts it to her righteousness or her father's righteousness looks to the general rule that the righteousness of the parent and child are interdependent. So a sinful man is not a suitable match for a righteous woman who is the daughter of a righteous man, but rather for a sinful woman who is the daughter of a sinful man. Similarly, a sinful woman is the daughter of a righteous man, so her father has no right to object, because the shame that befalls him from his daughter is greater than what befalls him from his son-in-law. However, if she is righteous and the daughter of a sinful man, and she gives herself in marriage to a sinful man, then he has no right to object, because he is like him, and she has consented to him. As verified in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His statement: 'even if he does not' – the waw is for connection, i.e., even if the sinful man is not openly sinning, i.e., not publicizing and manifesting it. This is the apparent meaning, because the shame from sin is not limited to open sinning. So it is in al-Nahr. [3] His statement: 'According to some scholars' – the purpose of it is to mention the benefit of the author's restriction of the ruling to generalization by his statement: 'in choosing the most excellent.'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya discusses the ruling on kafa'ah (suitability) with regard to religiosity and sinful behavior. The base ruling is that a sinful man (fasiq) is not a suitable match (kuf') for the daughter of a righteous man, even if the fasiq does not openly sin. The commentary then addresses three points. First, the phrase 'daughter of the righteous' raises the question of whether righteousness is attributed to the father or the woman herself. The author notes that in al-Majma', it is the woman's own righteousness that matters, while in al-Khaniyya, both the woman and her father must be righteous. The correct view (al-haqq) is that all are considered, and the apparent reasoning is that righteousness of parent and child are generally interdependent. Practical consequence: a fasiq man is only a match for a sinful woman of sinful parentage. If a sinful woman is the daughter of a righteous man, her father cannot object to her marrying a fasiq because the shame to him from her is greater. But if a righteous woman is the daughter of a fasiq, and she marries a fasiq, her father cannot object because he is himself similar. Second, the phrase 'even if he does not openly sin' means that non-open sinning does not alter the ruling, because the defect of sinfulness is not contingent on publicity; the disgrace is present regardless. Third, the mention of 'according to some scholars' is to show that the author al-Wiqaya restricted the ruling to the choice of the most excellent in religion and wealth, implying that if there is no better match, the fasiq might be considered suitable. This is a nuance.

Key Terms
فاسق (fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking religious integrity.
كفؤ (kuf'): A suitable match in marriage, assessed by lineage, profession, wealth, piety, and freedom.
إعلان (iʿlān): Openly committing sin, making it known to others.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, is a fasiq who does not openly sin considered a suitable match for a pious woman?
A) Yes, because his sin is not public.
B) No, because the disgrace of sin is not limited to open sinning.
C) Yes, if the woman's father consents.
D) No, only if he is a habitual sinner.

Key Takeaway
A fasiq is never a suitable match for a pious woman, even if his sin is hidden, because the disgrace of sin is inherent and not dependent on publicity.

Inability to Pay Prompt Mahr and Maintenance Negates Kafa'ah
Ruling with commentary on financial incapability and kafa'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how inability to pay prompt dower and maintenance affects kafa'ah, and identify the relied-upon view on the required duration of maintenance.

Core Idea
A man unable to pay prompt mahr and nafaqah is not kuf' for any woman, even a poor one.

In this passage, the author explains a key factor in kafa'ah: financial capability. The ruling seems straightforward—a man who cannot fulfill his financial obligations is not a suitable match. But the text clarifies an important nuance: the restriction 'for the poor woman' is meant to prevent the misconception that a poor man could be a match for a poor woman. In fact, he is not, and a wealthy woman is even more mismatched.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the core ruling: a man unable to pay the prompt dower and maintenance is not a suitable match (kuf') for a poor woman.
The text uses 'faqīrah' (poor woman) explicitly.
2
Understand the a fortiori reasoning
Recognize that the same ruling applies to a wealthy woman with even stronger reason due to greater disgrace.
The phrase 'wa kadha al-ghaniyyah bi al-tarīq al-ūlā' indicates this.
3
Distinguish prompt vs deferred dower
Note that only the prompt portion (mu'ajjal) matters; inability to pay deferred dower does not affect kafa'ah.
The deferred dower is not immediately due.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on maintenance duration
Identify the different views on how much maintenance the man must be able to provide: one month, six months, one year, or daily earning. Note the relied-upon view.
The mufta bihi is one month for non-craftsmen, daily earning for craftsmen.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a man who cannot pay the prompt dower but can work daily. Determine his kafa'ah status according to the relied-upon view.
If he can earn enough daily, he is considered capable for nafaqah.

So the one unable to pay the prompt dower and maintenance is not a suitable match for the poor woman. He only said 'for the poor woman' to repel the misconception of one who thought that a poor man would be a suitable match for a poor woman, and likewise for the wealthy woman a fortiori, because inability to pay the obligatory dower and maintenance is realized in him with the added disgrace. And it excludes the deferred dower, because inability to pay it at present does not harm kafa'ah. [2] His statement: 'and maintenance' – i.e., the wife's maintenance that is obligatory upon him. In al-Mujtaba, it is considered sufficient to be able to earn it. So he indicated by this restriction that he is not a suitable match for her, and it is understood that he is not a suitable match for the wealthy woman a fortiori, because the disgrace is greater. [4] His statement: 'with the added disgrace' – the iḍāfa is explanatory, i.e., added disgrace. This meaning is not present in the case of the poor woman, because she is not disgraced by her husband's poverty, being like her.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage discusses the factor of wealth in kafa'ah. The ruling: a man who cannot pay the prompt dower (mahr mu'ajjal) and the obligatory maintenance (nafaqah) is not a suitable match even for a poor woman, let alone a wealthy one. The author explains that the restriction 'for the poor woman' is to counter the assumption that a poor man would be a match for a poor woman. In fact, he is not, because the inability to fulfill the financial obligations is itself a defect. Moreover, the disgrace is greater for a wealthy woman, so she would suffer more shame. The commentary then clarifies three points. First, the prompt dower refers to the portion that is customarily paid immediately, even if the whole dower is considered due; the deferred part (mu'ajjal) does not affect kafa'ah because it is not due immediately. Second, regarding maintenance, scholars differ on the duration one must be able to provide. The most reliable view is one month for someone who is not a craftsman; if he is a craftsman, then the ability to earn enough each day suffices. Third, the phrase 'with the added disgrace' refers to the additional shame a wealthy woman would feel, which does not apply in the case of a poor woman who is used to poverty. The passage also notes that wealth as a criterion for kafa'ah applies to Arabs as well, contrary to some opinions.

Key Terms
مهر معجل (mahr mu'ajjal): The part of the dower that is customarily paid promptly at the time of marriage, even if the entire dower is considered due.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance required for the wife, including food, clothing, and shelter.
فقيرة (faqīrah): A poor woman who lacks wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only for a wealthy woman.
B) Only for a poor woman.
C) For both a poor and a wealthy woman, but the text emphasizes the poor woman to avoid misunderstanding.
D) For neither, but the ruling is only for non-Arabs.

Key Takeaway
Inability to pay prompt dower and maintenance makes a man non-kuf' for any woman, with the mufta bihi being one month of maintenance for non-craftsmen and daily earning for craftsmen.

The Poor Man's Kafa'ah: Inability to Pay Mahr and Nafaqah
Commentary on the ruling that a poor man is not a kuf' for any woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a man's inability to pay immediate mahr and one month's nafaqah negates his kafa'ah, and identify the relied-upon position regarding the duration of nafaqah.

Core Idea
A poor man is not a kuf' for any woman—even if she is poor—if he cannot pay immediate mahr and at least one month's nafaqah.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a crucial nuance in the Hanafi law of kafa'ah. One might think that a poor man is suitable for a poor woman due to equality in poverty, but the text refutes this, explaining that inability to fulfill financial obligations makes a man unsuitable for any woman. The commentator also details the internal Hanafi disagreement on the required period of maintenance capacity, with the preferred view being one month.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary ruling
Read the first sentence: 'The one unable to pay the immediate mahr and the maintenance is not a kuf' for the poor woman.' This is the core ruling.
Note that it specifically mentions 'poor woman' to preempt a misunderstanding.
2
Understand the reason for specifying 'poor woman'
The commentator explains that the text mentions 'poor woman' to dispel the notion that a poor man could be a kuf' for a poor woman due to equality in poverty. By a fortiori reasoning, he is also not a kuf' for a wealthy woman.
The key point: kafa'ah is about ability to fulfill obligations, not just social equality.
3
Analyze the definition of immediate mahr
Immediate mahr (mahr mu'ajjal) is what is customarily paid promptly, even if the entire dower is due now. Deferred mahr is not relevant for kafa'ah.
This prevents disputes: if a man can only pay a small immediate portion but can defer the rest, it does not affect kafa'ah.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on nafaqah duration
There are multiple views: one month, six months, or one year. The corrected view in al-Tajnīs is one month. For a professional (craftsman), the criterion is daily earning capacity.
This impacts whether a man is considered kuf' based on his ability to maintain a wife short-term.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn stating that a man unable to pay the immediate mahr and obligatory maintenance is not a suitable match (kuf') for a poor woman. The commentator clarifies why the text specifically mentions 'for the poor woman': it is to preempt the misconception that a poor man could be equal to a poor woman. In fact, he is not equal even to a poor woman, and by a fortiori reasoning, he is even less equal to a wealthy woman because of the added disgrace. The ability considered is for the immediate mahr (what is customarily paid promptly) and for maintenance. Regarding maintenance, there are different views within the Hanafi school: some consider ability for one month, others for six months or a year. The preferred view (al-sahih) is one month for a non-professional, and daily earnings for a professional. The commentator also cites that kafa'ah in wealth is considered even among Arabs, as verified in al-Bada'i' and al-Bahr.

مهر معجل (mahr mu'ajjal): The portion of the dower that is customarily paid immediately at marriage, even if the entire dower is due now.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance of the wife, including food, clothing, and shelter, obligatory upon the husband.
كفاءة (kafa'ah): Equality in social status and ability between spouses, a condition for the binding nature of marriage.
عاجر (ˈājiz): One who is incapable or unable to fulfill an obligation.

Kafa'ah: Wealth and Lowly Professions in Hanafi Marriage Law
Explanation of wealth and profession in kafa'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on wealth and lowly professions in kafa'ah and distinguish between the mufta bihi position and the contrary narration from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Wealth is transient; only the ability to pay matters, but lowly professions disqualify based on custom.

In determining suitability (kafa'ah) for marriage, the Hanafi school holds that wealth is not a criterion because it fluctuates. However, having a lowly profession (ḥirfah dāni'ah) does affect kafa'ah. This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah explains the reasoning and highlights an intra-madhhab difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of kafa'ah discussed
The passage covers both wealth (al-yaṣār) and profession (al-ḥirfah). Recognize that each is treated differently: wealth is generally not considered, while profession is considered based on custom.
2
Analyze the ruling on wealth
The text states that a man capable of paying mahr and nafaqah is kuf' for a woman of great wealth. This is the correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ). The reason given is that wealth 'comes and goes' (ghādī wa rā'iḥ), so it is not a stable criterion.
Wealth is only relevant if its absence prevents fulfilling obligations.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There is a narration from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that wealth should be considered because people boast about it. The text rejects this as not the authoritative view. The relied-upon position is in the ẓāhir al-riwāyah.
This ikhtilaf is non-ʿUṣūlī (ghayr riwāyat al-uṣūl).
4
Learn the ruling on lowly professions
The text lists examples: weaver, cupper, sweeper, tanner. The commentary clarifies that the criterion is custom (ʿurf): whatever society deems lowly (danī’) affects kafa'ah.
For non-Arabs and Arabs who adopt such professions, they are considered.
5
Understand the practical difference (thamarah)
If wealth were a criterion, a poor but able man might not be suitable for a wealthy woman. The Hanafi view allows the marriage if he can pay, preventing obstruction based on mere wealth disparity.

والقادر عليهما كفو لذاتِ أموال عظيمة ، هو الصحيح (۱) ) ؛ لأنَّ المال غادِ ورائح، فلا يعتبر بعدمِهِ إلا أن يكون بحيث لا يَقْدِرُ على أداء الواجب، وهو المهر والنفقة.

Book of Marriage: Chapter of the Guardian and the Kuf'. And the one capable of both (mahr and nafaqah) is a kuf' for a woman of great wealth; that is the correct view. And (he is not a kuf' if he has) a lowly profession: a weaver, or a cupper, or a sweeper, or a tanner. [2] And the one capable of both is a kuf' for a woman of great wealth; that is the correct view, because wealth comes and goes, so it is not considered by its absence unless he is unable to fulfill the obligation, which is mahr and nafaqah. (And his statement: a lowly profession: a weaver, or a cupper, or a sweeper, or a tanner) [1] His statement: 'a kuf' for a woman of great wealth' means that kafa'ah is not considered in affluence and equality in wealth, such that a person of little wealth would be a kuf' for a woman of much wealth. [2] His statement: 'that is the correct view' is to exclude what was narrated from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: that kafa'ah in affluence is considered, because people boast about little or much wealth. [3] His statement: 'goes and comes' from al-ghadwah (morning) and al-rawāh (afternoon), and it refers to the fly after noon, and its gist is that wealth does not remain with a person; how many are rich in the morning and poor in the evening, and vice versa. So its absence is not considered unless it reaches the level of inability to perform the obligation. [4] His statement: 'and profession' with kasr of the ha: this is considered for non-Arabs and Arabs if they adopt professions and boast about them like non-Arabs. [0] His statement: 'or a cupper': the cupper (hajjām) with fatha on the ha and shadda on the jim is one who earns through cupping; in Persian: shākh kash. Al-kannās similarly is one who earns by removing dirt with a broom etc. Al-dabbāgh similarly is one who tans leather. Al-‘attār is one who sells perfume. Al-bazzāz is a seller of cloth. Al-sarrāf is one who exchanges dirhams and dinars. And the criterion in this chapter is custom: whatever people of custom consider lowly is lowly. (1) A caution against what was narrated from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad in  غير رواية الأصول that kafa'ah in affluence is considered, because people boast about little or much wealth. See Rumūz al-Ḥaqā'iq (1:149).

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on kafa'ah (suitability) in marriage. It first states that a man who is capable of paying both the mahr and nafaqah is a suitable match (kuf') even for a woman of immense wealth. This is the correct view (al-sahih) in the Hanafi school, contrary to a narration from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that wealth should be considered. The reasoning is that wealth is transient – 'it comes and goes' – so it is not a stable criterion. The only time wealth matters is when a person is unable to fulfill the obligatory financial duties. The passage then addresses lowly professions (حرفة). The matn lists examples: a weaver (ḥā'ik), cupper (ḥajjām), sweeper (kannās), and tanner (dabbāgh). The commentary explains that these professions are considered lowly based on custom ('urf). For non-Arabs and Arabs who adopt these professions, they affect kafa'ah. The commentary also notes other professions like perfumer, cloth seller, and money changer, but the criterion is ultimately what society deems lowly.

Key Terms
غاد ورائح (ghādī wa rā'iḥ): Literal: going in the morning and coming in the afternoon; metaphor for the transient nature of wealth.
حرفة (ḥirfah): A lowly craft or occupation that affects kafa'ah in marriage.
دنيء (danī’): Lowly or base; what society considers a low-status occupation.

A) Because wealth is prohibited in Islam.
B) Because wealth fluctuates and is not permanent.
C) Because all people are equal regardless of wealth.
D) Because the Quran commands ignoring wealth.

Key Takeaway
Wealth is not a criterion for kafa'ah due to its transience, but lowly professions as determined by custom are disqualifying.

Kafa'ah: Wealth Not a Condition Except for Wajibat
Ruling on kafa'ah in wealth with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether wealth is a condition for kafa'ah and explain why the mufta bihi view disregards wealth except for ability to pay wajibat.

Core Idea
Wealth is not a condition for kafa'ah because it is transient—only ability to pay wajibat matters.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute about the role of wealth in marital suitability (kafa'ah). While some early transmissions from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad suggest wealth should be considered because people boast about it, the relied-upon position declares wealth irrelevant due to its impermanence. Understanding this ruling clarifies that a man of modest means can be a suitable match for a wealthy woman, provided he can afford the obligatory mahr and nafaqah.

Guided Path
1
State the main Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: a man capable of paying mahr and nafaqah is suitable (kufu) even for a woman of great wealth. Wealth itself is not a condition.
This is the 'sahih' (correct) view.
2
Identify the variant view
Note that some transmissions attribute to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad the consideration of wealth (yasar) in kafa'ah because people pride themselves on wealth.
These transmissions are not from authoritative sources (ghayr riwayat al-usul).
3
Analyze the dalil for the main view
The proof is rational: wealth is transient ('ghadin wa ra'ih'—coming and going). It cannot be a stable basis for suitability. The only exception is when inability to pay wajibat (mahr, nafaqah) renders a man unsuitable.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The commentator declares the main view 'as-sahih' and states it excludes (ihtiraz) the variant report. The mufta bihi is the position that does not consider wealth except for wajibat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب الولي والكفؤ
والقادر عليهما كفو لذاتِ أموال عظيمة ، هو الصحيح (۱) )؛ لأنَّ المال غادِ ورائح، فلا يعتبر بعدمِهِ إلا أن يكون بحيث لا يَقْدِرُ على أداء الواجب، وهو المهر والنفقة.
[١] قوله : كفء لذات أموال عظيمة ؛ حاصله أنه لا تعتبر الكفاءة في اليسار والمساواة في الغنى، حتى يكون صاحب المال القليل كفؤاً لذات أموال كثيرة. [٢] قوله : هو الصحيح ؛ احتراز عما روي عن أبي حنيفة ومحمد : أن الكفاءة في اليسار معتبرة ؛ لأن الناس يتفاخرون بقلة المال وكثرته.
[٣]قوله: غاد؛ من الغدو والروائح من الرواح، وهو الذباب بعد الزوال، وحاصله : إن المال لا يستمر في شخص، فكم من غني صباحاً فقير مساء وبالعكس، فلا يعتبر عدمه إلا أن يبلغ مبلغ العجز عن أداء الواجب.
Book of Nikah, Chapter of Guardian and Suitability. And the one who is capable of both is suitable for a woman of great wealth; that is the correct view, because wealth comes and goes, so it is not considered by its absence except when he is unable to fulfill the obligatory, which is the mahr and maintenance. His saying: 'suitable for a woman of great wealth'; its summary is that suitability in prosperity and equality in wealth is not considered, such that a man of little wealth is suitable for a woman of great wealth. His saying: 'that is the correct view'; it excludes what is narrated from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that suitability in prosperity is considered because people boast about little or much wealth. His saying: 'coming and going'; from 'ghuduww' (morning) and 'rawah' (afternoon), and its sum is that wealth does not remain with a person, so many a rich in the morning is poor in the evening and vice versa, so its absence is not considered except when it reaches the point of inability to perform the obligatory.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether wealth (yasar) is a condition for kafa'ah (marital suitability). The author begins by stating the ruling: a man who is capable of providing mahr and nafaqah is suitable for a woman of great wealth; this is the correct view (as-sahih). He explains that wealth is not a factor because it is transient—people gain and lose it daily. However, if a man cannot afford the wajibat (mahr and nafaqah), then he is not suitable, as the inability directly affects the marital obligations. This ruling is a refutation (ihtiraz) of a reported view from Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that yasar is considered because people boast about it. The text clarifies that this reported view is not from the authoritative sources (ghayr riwayat al-usul), and the mufta bihi position is the one stated. The proof is rational: the temporary nature of wealth. This position is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon fatwa.

Key Terms
كفء (kufu): Equal in status and qualifications for marriage, specifically in lineage, religion, profession, and ability to provide.
يسار (yasar): Wealth or financial prosperity.
واجب (wajib): Obligatory financial duties in marriage, specifically mahr and nafaqah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, wealth is always a condition.
B) Never, wealth is never considered.
C) Only if the woman is extremely wealthy.
D) Only when the man cannot afford the wajibat (mahr and nafaqah).

Key Takeaway
Wealth is not a condition for kafa'ah in the Hanafi school; only the inability to pay mahr and nafaqah can render a man unsuitable.

Profession-Based Kafa'ah in Hanafi Nikah
Ruling on profession-based kafa'ah with definitions and multiple opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on profession-based kafa'ah, identify the two categories of professions, and explain how custom ('urf) influences suitability.

Core Idea
Low professions are not kufu to high professions; custom determines which professions are low.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah expands the ruling on kafa'ah regarding professions. It presents the core rule that certain lowly trades are not suitable matches for higher ones, then explores different scholarly opinions on how closely professions must match and the role of local custom. Understanding this topic requires grasping the Hanafi reliance on 'urf and the existence of legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read the first line: a weaver, cupper, sweeper, tanner is not a suitable match (kufu) for a perfumer, cloth merchant, or money changer. This is the mufta bihi.
2
Recognize the two-category division
From Multaqa and Zahidi: crafts are divided into low and high. Individuals within each category are kufu to each other, but not across categories.
The low category includes weaver, cupper, sweeper, tanner, etc.; the high includes perfumer, cloth merchant, money changer.
3
Examine the intra-school ikhtilaf
Al-Bahr says strict identity is not needed; closeness suffices (e.g., weaver kufu to cupper). Al-Fath says the criterion is 'urf: if a profession is not considered low, it can be kufu. Note the example of Alexandria.
This flexibility shows the role of custom in determining suitability.
4
Understand the mufta bihi and the role of 'urf
The author affirms that the fatwa is to consider profession. The underlying basis is 'urf, which can vary by time and place. The al-Fath view illustrates that 'urf can override a fixed classification.
Thus, the ruling is not static; it adapts to societal norms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحرفة، فحائك، أو حجام، أو كناس، أو دباغ ليس بكفؤ لعطّار، أو بزاز، أو صراف، وبه يفتى
[١]قوله : ليس بكفؤ ؛ قال في «ملتقى الأبحر» و «شرحه»: فحائك أو حجام، أو كناس، أو دباغ، أو حلاق، أو بيطار، أو حداد، أو صفار غير كفء لسائر الحرف : كعاطر أو بَزاز أو صراف، وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحرف جنسان ليس أحدهما كفؤاً للآخر ، لكن أفراد كل منهما كفء لجنسها، وبه يفتي، «زاهدي». وذكر في «البحر»: أنه لا يلزم اتحادهما في الحرفة، بل التقارب كاف، فالحائك كف للحجام، والدباغ كف للكناس، والصفاء كفء للحداد، والعطار للبزاز. وذكر في «الفتح»: إن الموجب هو استنقاص أهل العرف، فيدور معه، وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يكون الحائك كفؤاً للعطّار بالاسكندرية لما ينالُ من حسن اعتبارها ، وعدم عدها نقصاً، اللهم إلا أن تقترن به خساسة أخرى (۳).
وفي «البناية»: «عن «الغاية»: الكنَّاس والحَجام والدباغ والحارس والسائس والراعي والقيم : أي البلان في الحَمَّام ليس كف لبنت الخياط، ولا الخياط لبنت البزاز والتاجر، ولا هما لبنت عالم وقاض ، والحائك ليس كفؤاً لبنت الدهقان) وإن كانت فقيرة (0)
[٢]قوله : وبه يفتى ؛ أي باعتبار الحرفة في الكفاءة، وهو احتراز عن قول عدم
And [the ruling on] craft: a weaver, or cupper, or sweeper, or tanner is not a suitable match for a perfumer, or cloth-merchant, or money-changer, and according to this the fatwa is given. His saying: 'not suitable'; it is said in Multaqa al-Abhar and its commentary: a weaver, cupper, sweeper, tanner, barber, veterinary, blacksmith, or coppersmith is not suitable for other crafts like perfumer, cloth-merchant, or money-changer. And in it is an indication that crafts are of two types: one is not suitable for the other, but individuals within each type are suitable for its type, and according to this the fatwa is given, from Zahidi. And it is mentioned in al-Bahr that it is not necessary that they be identical in craft, rather closeness is sufficient: the weaver is suitable for the cupper, the tanner for the sweeper, the coppersmith for the blacksmith, the perfumer for the cloth-merchant. And it is mentioned in al-Fath that the effective cause is the disparagement of the people of custom, so it revolves with it. On this basis, a weaver should be suitable for a perfumer in Alexandria due to the good regard and not considering it a deficiency, unless another baseness is attached to it. And in al-Binayah from al-Ghayah: the sweeper, cupper, tanner, watchman, groom, shepherd, and the bath attendant (the cleaner in the bathhouse) is not suitable for the daughter of a tailor, nor the tailor for the daughter of a cloth-merchant or merchant, nor both for the daughter of a scholar or judge; the weaver is not suitable for the daughter of a village chief, even if she is poor. His saying: 'and according to this the fatwa is given' means with consideration of the craft in suitability, and it is a safeguard against the statement of non-consideration.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on kafa'ah concerning professions. The author states the core rule: certain lowly professions (weaver, cupper, sweeper, tanner) are not considered equal (kufu) to higher professions (perfumer, cloth merchant, money changer). This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). The commentary elaborates by citing multiple sources. From 'Multaqa al-Abhar' and its commentary, the list is expanded to include barber, veterinary, blacksmith, coppersmith, and it notes that crafts are divided into two categories: low and high. Individuals within each category are kufu to each other, but not across categories. However, 'al-Bahr' states that strict identity is not required; closeness is sufficient, providing examples of cross-suitability within the low category (e.g., weaver for cupper). 'al-Fath' introduces a key principle: the criterion is the custom of the people ('urf)—what is considered low in a particular society. For instance, in Alexandria, a weaver might be considered suitable for a perfumer if the profession is not looked down upon. 'al-Binayah' adds further hierarchies: certain low professions are not suitable even for daughters of tailors, and tailors are not suitable for merchants or scholars, indicating a gradation. The passage concludes by affirming that the fatwa is based on considering the profession, not ignoring it. The underlying basis is 'urf (custom), which can vary by time and place.

Key Terms
حرفة (hirafah): A craft or profession, considered in kafa'ah. Low professions are those considered base by custom.
عرف (urf): Custom or common practice of the people, which determines whether a profession is considered low or high.
دناءة (dana'ah): Lowliness or baseness of a profession, as judged by societal custom.
كفاءة (kafa'ah): Equality or suitability in marriage, considering factors like lineage, profession, wealth, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a low profession?
A) Perfumer ('attār)
B) Cloth merchant (bazzāz)
C) Money changer (sarrāf)
D) Tanner (dabbāgh)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi nikah, profession is a valid factor in kafa'ah, with low professions not equal to high professions, and custom ('urf) determines the classification.

Dower Deficiency and the Guardian's Right to Object
First ruling on deficient dower

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a woman marries for less than her proper dower and explain the guardian's right to object and seek completion or separation.

Core Idea
A marriage for deficient dower is valid but the guardian may object until the husband completes the mahr or the qadi separates them.

The first ruling in this passage addresses a scenario where a woman marries herself for a dower less than what is customary (mahr al-mithl). The Hanafi school holds that the marriage contract itself is sahih (valid), but the guardian (عصبة) has a legally recognized right to intervene. This lesson unpacks that right and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Initial Ruling
The act is a woman marrying herself with a deficient dower. The ruling is that the contract is valid (sahih).
The validity of the contract itself is not affected by the deficiency.
2
Locate the Guardian's Right
The text says 'فللولي الاعتراض' – the guardian has the right to object. This is a specific legal capacity (haqq al-iraadah).
The guardian cannot annul unilaterally; he must seek completion or separation through the qadi.
3
Specify the Guardian's Options
The guardian may demand that the husband complete the dower to the proper amount (mahr al-mithl) or, if that is not done, seek a judicial separation.
The separation is with the qadi's order, not self-help.
4
Contrast with Other Rulings
Note that this is not a case of the contract being fasid or batil; it is valid but subject to objection.
This is a distinct Hanafi position; the Shafi'i school does not recognize a guardian's objection in this case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن نكحت بأقل من مهرها فللولي الاعتراض حتى يتم أو يفرق. ووقف نكاح فضولي أو فضوليين على الإجازة. ويتولى طرفي النكاح واحد ليس بفضولي من جانب : أي يتولي واحد الإيجاب والقبول، ولا يشترط أن يتكلّم بهما، فإنَّ الواحد إذا كان وكيلاً منهما، فقال: زوجتُها إِيَّاهُ كان كافيا، وهو على أقسام : إما أن يكون أصيلاً ووليا . أو أصيلاً ووكيلاً . أو ولياً من الجانبين . أو وكيلاً من الجانبين.
And if she marries for less than her mahr (i.e., less than the mahr of her peers), then the guardian has the right to object until he completes (the mahr) or separates. And the marriage contract of a fuduli or two fudulis is suspended pending ratification. And one person performs both sides of the marriage contract while not being a fuduli from either side, meaning he performs the offer and acceptance in one utterance, and it is not required that he speaks both separately. Indeed, if one is an agent from both parties and says, 'I have married her to him,' it is sufficient. He is of categories: either he is a principal and a guardian, or a principal and an agent, or a guardian from both sides, or an agent from both sides.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from the Hanafi school regarding marriage contracts. First, if a woman marries herself for less than her proper dower (mahr al-mithl), her guardian (the عصبة) has the right to object. He may demand that the husband complete the dower or seek a separation through the qadi. The marriage itself is not invalid but is subject to the guardian's objection. Second, if an unauthorized person (fuduli) performs the marriage contract, whether from one side or both, the contract is suspended (موقوف) and requires ratification (الإجازة) from the principal. Without ratification, the contract does not take effect. Third, a single individual may perform both the offer and acceptance in a marriage contract, provided he is not a fuduli from either side. He may be an agent for both parties, or a guardian for both, or an agent from one side and principal/guardian from the other. The four valid scenarios are listed: (1) principal and guardian, (2) principal and agent, (3) guardian from both sides, (4) agent from both sides. The text emphasizes that the one performing both sides must have valid authority from each party.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower of a woman's peers, used as a benchmark for deficiency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The marriage is invalid and must be annulled.

C) The marriage is valid but the guardian may still object later.
D) The marriage is suspended until the guardian ratifies.

Key Takeaway
A marriage with deficient dower is valid, but the guardian has the right to demand completion or judicial separation.

Fuduli Marriage: Suspension and Ratification
Second ruling on fuduli marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a fuduli marriage, explain that it is suspended (mawquf), and identify the effect of ratification (ijazah).

Core Idea
A marriage by an unauthorized intermeddler (fuduli) is not invalid but suspended, awaiting ratification by the principal.

The second ruling introduces the concept of the fuduli (unauthorized intermeddler) in marriage contracts. The Hanafi school treats a contract made by a fuduli, whether on one side or both, as suspended (mawquf)—neither fully valid nor automatically void. This lesson clarifies the legal status and the role of ratification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Agent
A fuduli is someone who acts for another without permission or legal authority. The contract is made without the principal's consent.
May be from one side (e.g., a stranger offers on behalf of the woman) or both sides.
2
State the Legal Status
The text says 'ووقف نكاح فضولي أو فضوليين على الإجازة' – the marriage is suspended (mawquf) pending ratification.
Mawquf means the contract has no immediate effect; it is not void but incomplete.
3
Define Ratification
Al-ijazah is retroactive ratification by the principal. Once given, the contract becomes valid from inception.
The principal may be the woman herself (if she is adult) or her guardian if needed.
4
Contrast with Invalid Contracts
A fuduli contract is not batil; it can become sahih through ratification. This distinguishes it from contracts with fundamental defects.
If ratification is denied, the contract is void ab initio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن نكحت بأقل من مهرها فللولي الاعتراض حتى يتم أو يفرق. ووقف نكاح فضولي أو فضوليين على الإجازة. ويتولى طرفي النكاح واحد ليس بفضولي من جانب : أي يتولي واحد الإيجاب والقبول، ولا يشترط أن يتكلّم بهما، فإنَّ الواحد إذا كان وكيلاً منهما، فقال: زوجتُها إِيَّاهُ كان كافيا، وهو على أقسام : إما أن يكون أصيلاً ووليا . أو أصيلاً ووكيلاً . أو ولياً من الجانبين . أو وكيلاً من الجانبين.
And if she marries for less than her mahr (i.e., less than the mahr of her peers), then the guardian has the right to object until he completes (the mahr) or separates. And the marriage contract of a fuduli or two fudulis is suspended pending ratification. And one person performs both sides of the marriage contract while not being a fuduli from either side, meaning he performs the offer and acceptance in one utterance, and it is not required that he speaks both separately. Indeed, if one is an agent from both parties and says, 'I have married her to him,' it is sufficient. He is of categories: either he is a principal and a guardian, or a principal and an agent, or a guardian from both sides, or an agent from both sides.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from the Hanafi school regarding marriage contracts. First, if a woman marries herself for less than her proper dower (mahr al-mithl), her guardian (the عصبة) has the right to object. He may demand that the husband complete the dower or seek a separation through the qadi. The marriage itself is not invalid but is subject to the guardian's objection. Second, if an unauthorized person (fuduli) performs the marriage contract, whether from one side or both, the contract is suspended (موقوف) and requires ratification (الإجازة) from the principal. Without ratification, the contract does not take effect. Third, a single individual may perform both the offer and acceptance in a marriage contract, provided he is not a fuduli from either side. He may be an agent for both parties, or a guardian for both, or an agent from one side and principal/guardian from the other. The four valid scenarios are listed: (1) principal and guardian, (2) principal and agent, (3) guardian from both sides, (4) agent from both sides. The text emphasizes that the one performing both sides must have valid authority from each party.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended contract pending ratification; not void but not fully effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a fuduli marriage contract if the principal rejects it?
A) It remains suspended indefinitely.
B) It becomes automatically valid.
C) It becomes void (batil).
D) It requires a qadi's decision.

Key Takeaway
A marriage by a fuduli is suspended until the principal ratifies or rejects it.

One Person Performing Both Sides of the Marriage Contract
Third ruling on single-person contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid scenarios in which a single person can perform both offer and acceptance in a marriage contract and explain why he must not be a fuduli from any side.

Core Idea
One person can perform both sides of a marriage contract only if he has valid authority from both parties and is not a fuduli.

The third ruling addresses a practical question: can one individual handle both the offer and acceptance in a marriage contract? The Hanafi school permits this provided that the person is not a fuduli from any side, meaning he must have proper authority to act for both parties. This lesson breaks down the four valid categories and the condition of non-fuduli status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Permissible Agent
The text says 'ويتولى طرفي النكاح واحد ليس بفضولي من جانب' – one person who is not a fuduli from either side can perform both sides.
This means he must have valid authority (guardianship or agency) for both parties.
2
Recognize the Four Valid Categories
The text lists four permissible combinations: (1) principal and guardian, (2) principal and agent, (3) guardian from both sides, (4) agent from both sides.
The person cannot be a fuduli for either party (i.e., must have authorization).
3
Understand the Form of Contract
The person does not need to utter separate offer and acceptance; a single statement like 'I have married her to him' suffices if he is agent for both.
This is a practical concession for convenience.
4
Contrast with the Fuduli Scenario
If a person is a fuduli for even one side (e.g., acts without permission for the woman), he cannot validly perform both sides. That would fall under the fuduli ruling.
Abu Yusuf reportedly differed on this; but the relied-upon position is that it is invalid.

One Person Performing Both Sides of the Marriage Contract
Conditions for one person to perform ijab and qabul in nikah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which one person can perform both sides of a marriage contract and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the need for principal's acceptance when the husband is not a suitable match.

Core Idea
A single person can perform ijab and qabul in nikah only if not a fuduli; Abu Hanifa holds it valid immediately while the Sahibayn require acceptance.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the rules for when one person can handle both the offer and acceptance in a marriage contract. It covers the permissible scenarios and highlights a key disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students regarding the validity of the contract when the husband is not a suitable match.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The text states that one person can manage both sides of the marriage provided he is not a fuduli (unauthorized agent) from any side.
2
Enumerate the permissible scenarios
List the five divisions: agent from both sides, guardian from both sides, agent from one side and principal from the other, guardian from one side and principal from the other, agent from one side and guardian from the other.
3
Distinguish the fuduli scenarios
A fuduli (from one or both sides) cannot perform both sides; this is contrary to Abu Yusuf's view.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa says the contract is valid and executed immediately without awaiting acceptance. The Sahibayn say it is not valid unless the principal accepts, because the absolute is understood according to custom (marrying a suitable match).
The passage states 'وصح' (and it is valid) attributing to Abu Hanifa.
5
Determine the practical difference
Under Abu Hanifa, the marriage is valid immediately; under the Sahibayn, it is suspended (mawquf) until the principal accepts. The relied-upon opinion is Abu Hanifa's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتولى طرفي النكاح واحد ليس بفضولي من جانب) : أي يتولي واحد الإيجاب والقبول، ولا يشترط أن يتكلّم بهما، فإنَّ الواحد إذا كان وكيلاً منهما، فقال: زوجتُها إِيَّاهُ كان كافيا، وهو على أقسام :
إما أن يكون أصيلاً وَولْيا، كابن العم يزوج بنت عمه الصغيرة.
أو أصيلاً ووكيلاً كما إذا وكلت رجلاً بأن يزوجها نفسه، فزوجها من نفسه.
أو ولياً من الجانبين، كالجد يزوج لابنِ ابنه بنتِ ابنه الآخر، وليس لهما.
أو وكيلاً من الجانبين.
(قوله : ليس بفضولي؛ الجملة صفةٌ لواحد : يعني إن تولّي طرفي النكاح من واحد، وأداء الإيجاب والقبول بكلام واحد، إنما يكون إذا لم يكن ذلك الواحد فضولياً من جانب من الجانبين.
قال في «كشف الوقاية»: الأقسام المحتملة بينها خمسة :
١ . إما أن يكون وكيلاً من الجانبين.
٢. أو ولياً منهما.
أو وكيلاً من جانب أصيلاً من جانب.
٤ أو ولياً من جانب أصيلاً من جانب.
ه . أو وكيلاً من جانب ولياً من جانب.
وإذا كان فضولياً في الجملة ليس له أن يتولى طرفي النكاح خلافاً لأبي يوسف، وهو أربعة أقسام : فضولي من جانب ووكيلاً من جانب، أو ولي أو أصيل من جانب، أو فضولي من الجانبين.
قوله : أو وكيلاً من الجانبين؛ بأن وكلت امرأة رجلاً بتزويجها من زيد مثلاً، ووكل زيد أيضاً ذلك الرجل بتزويجه منها، فيقولُ الوكيلُ زَوَّجتُ موكلي فلاناً موكلتي فلانة، ويكفي شاهدان على وكالتها ووكالته وعلى العقد؛ لأن الشاهد يتحملُ الشهادات العديدية، والشهادة على التوكيل ليست بلازمة، وإنما يحتاج إلـيـهـا عـنـد الجحود.
(وصح) : أي نفذ من غير توقف، وهذا عنده، وقالا : لا يصح إلا إذا أجازه الآمر؛ لأن المطلق ينصرف إلى المتعارف، وهو التزويج بالكفؤ.
And one person manages both sides of the marriage, not being a فضولي from a side. Meaning: one person performs the offer and acceptance, and it is not required that he speaks them separately. For if one is an agent from both sides and says 'I have married her to him,' it is sufficient. And it has divisions: (1) He is a principal and a guardian, like a cousin marrying his minor paternal uncle's daughter. (2) Or a principal and an agent, as when a woman appoints a man to marry her to himself, and he marries her to himself. (3) Or a guardian from both sides, like a grandfather marrying the daughter of his son to the son of his other son, when they have no guardians. (4) Or an agent from both sides. (His statement: 'not being a فضولي' is an attribute of 'one': meaning that one person managing both sides of the marriage and performing offer and acceptance with one speech is only possible if that person is not a فضولي from either side. He said in Kashf al-Wiqayah: The possible divisions are five: 1. Agent from both sides. 2. Guardian from both sides. 3. Agent from one side and principal from the other. 4. Guardian from one side and principal from the other. 5. Agent from one side and guardian from the other. And if he is a فضولي in general, he may not manage both sides of the marriage, contrary to Abu Yusuf. And it has four divisions: فضولي from one side and agent from the other, or guardian or principal from the other, or فضولي from both sides. His statement: 'or agent from both sides' is when a woman appoints a man to marry her to Zayd, for example, and Zayd also appoints that man to marry him to her. So the agent says: 'I have married my client so-and-so to my client so-and-so.' And two witnesses suffice for her agency and his agency and for the contract, because the witness bears multiple testimonies, and testimony about the appointment is not obligatory; it is only needed in case of denial. (And it is valid) meaning it is executed without being suspended. This is according to him (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is not valid unless the principal accepts it, because the absolute is understood according to custom, which is marrying a suitable match.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the ruling that one person can handle both the offer and acceptance in a marriage contract, provided that person is not a 'فضولي' (unauthorized agent) from either side. The author first states the general rule, then enumerates the permissible scenarios: the person can be an agent from both sides, a guardian from both sides, an agent from one side and principal from the other, a guardian from one side and principal from the other, or an agent from one side and guardian from the other. The commentary from Kashf al-Wiqayah clarifies that if the person is a فضولي in any capacity, he cannot perform both sides, and this is contrary to Abu Yusuf's view. The passage then gives an example of an agent from both sides and notes that two witnesses suffice for both agencies and the contract, as witnesses can bear multiple testimonies. Finally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the contract is valid and executed immediately (without awaiting acceptance), while his two students, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, require the principal's acceptance unless the husband is a suitable match (كفؤ). Abu Hanifa's argument is that the absolute command (to marry) is understood according to custom, and custom is that the agent marries the woman to a suitable match. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts without authorization (i.e., unauthorized agent) in a contract. In this context, a person who handles both sides of the marriage contract without being an agent or guardian from one or both sides.
وكيل (wakīl): An agent appointed by a principal to act on their behalf in the marriage contract.
ولي (walī): A legal guardian who has authority to give the bride in marriage. In this passage, the grandfather is given as an example of a guardian from both sides.
أصيل (aṣīl): A principal (the person on whose behalf the contract is made). When one person is both principal and agent, he acts for himself and as agent for the other party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he is an agent from both sides
B) When he is a guardian from both sides
C) When he is a فضولي from one or both sides
D) When he is a principal from one side and agent from the other

Key Takeaway
One person can handle both sides of a marriage if not a fuduli; Abu Hanifa holds it valid immediately, while his students require acceptance if the husband is not a suitable match.

The Prohibition of Dual Capacity in Marriage Agency
Prohibition of dual capacity in marriage agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four prohibited combinations of dual capacity when one person acts as a fuduli in a marriage contract and explain why each is prohibited.

Core Idea
A single person cannot be both fuduli and principal, guardian, or agent in the same marriage contract.

In Hanafi marriage law, a fuduli is someone who acts without prior authorization. But complications arise when the same person holds multiple roles—e.g., being both the one who commands and the one who acts without command. This lesson explores the prohibition of such dual capacity, focusing on four specific scenarios. Understanding these is essential before analyzing the validity dispute in the next lesson.

Guided Path
1
Define fuduli
A fuduli is a person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission, i.e., a volunteer agent.
2
List the prohibited combinations
The text lists four: (1) principal and fuduli, (2) guardian from one side and fuduli from the other, (3) agent from one side and fuduli from the other, (4) fuduli from both sides.
3
Identify the conflict in each combination
Acting as both principal and fuduli leads to self-contradiction; the other combos involve mixing authorized and unauthorized roles, creating confusion about authority.
4
State the ruling
It is not permissible (la yajuz) to contract marriage in any of these ways. The prohibition is agreed upon.
The validity of the contract if performed is a separate issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الولي والكفؤ
ه أو ولياً من جانب ووكيلاً من جانب. ولا يجوز أن يكون الواحد فضولياً كما إذا كان: أصيلاً وفضولياً، أو ولياً من جانب وفضولياً من جانب، أو وكيلاً من جانب وفضولياً من جانب، أو فضولياً من الجانبين. (وصح) أي نفذ من غير توقف، وهذا عنده، وقالا: لا يصح إلا إذا أجازه الآمر؛ لأن المطلق ينصرف إلى المتعارف، وهو التزويج بالكفؤ.
Chapter of the Guardian and Compatibility. This: or a guardian from one side and an agent from the other. It is not permissible for one person to be a volunteer, such as when he is: a principal and a volunteer, or a guardian from one side and a volunteer from the other, or an agent from one side and a volunteer from the other, or a volunteer from both sides. (And it is valid) meaning it takes effect without delay, and this is according to him (Abu Hanifa), and they (the two) said: it is not valid unless the one who commanded gives permission, because the absolute (word) is understood according to custom, which is marriage to a compatible match.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a marriage when one person acts in a dual capacity, such as being both the principal and the fuduli (voluntary agent) or guardian and fuduli, etc. The text first states that it is not permissible for a single person to act as a fuduli in these combinations. Then it addresses the ruling if such a marriage is contracted: According to Abu Hanifa, the marriage is valid (nāfidh) without requiring subsequent permission, because the authority to contract marriage is effective from the moment of the contract. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Sahibayn) hold that it is not valid unless the principal (the one who had the right to contract) gives permission afterward. Their reasoning is that when an absolute command is given, it is customarily understood to be restricted to what is conventional—i.e., marriage to a compatible match. The passage is an expansion (sharh) on the matn statement 'wa saḥḥa' (and it is valid).

Key Terms
فضولي (fudūlī): A person who acts on behalf of another without prior permission, i.e., a volunteer agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the prohibited dual capacities mentioned in the text?
A) Principal and fuduli
B) Guardian from one side and fuduli from the other
C) Agent from one side and fuduli from the other
D) Guardian from both sides

Key Takeaway
It is not permissible for one person to act as a fuduli in dual capacity in a marriage contract; there are four specific prohibited combinations.
